  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',

<span id="freeTextContainer13258272530600059966"><i>Lilith</i> is a story concerning the nature of life, death, and salvation.<br/><br/>After he followed the old man through the mirror, nothing in his life was ever right again.   <span id="freeText13258272530600059966" style="display:none"><i>Lilith</i> is a story concerning the nature of life, death, and salvation.<br /><br />After he followed the old man through the mirror, nothing in his life was ever right again. It was a special mirror and the man he followed was a special man - a man who led him to the things that underlie the fate of all creation. <i>Lilith</i> is considered among the darkest of MacDonald's works, and among the most profound. It is a story concerning the nature of life, death, and salvation. In the story, MacDonald mentions a cosmic sleep that heals tortured souls, preceding the salvation of all.</span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_2569108305" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2569108305" />
    <a title="Fergus" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/70395042-fergus"><img alt="Fergus" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1567548184p2/70395042.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2569108305?book_show_action=true">Oct 21, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fergus" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fergus" href="/user/show/70395042-fergus">Fergus</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2569108305" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16108780659342373929">When I was 2, my wonderful grandmother visited our little postwar stucco bungalow out on the fringes of our little former lumber town.<br/><br/>My grandmother believed in magic - White Magic. Back then she was like a Fairy Grandmother to me...<br/><br/>One day during her visit, she took me by the hand and led me down to our unfinished basement. All she said to me was - ‘Today we’re going to visit the Zoo.’<br/><br/>And she somehow, within my own private tiny toddler’s headspace, had suddenly transformed our little subterrane</span>
  <span id="freeText16108780659342373929" style="display:none">When I was 2, my wonderful grandmother visited our little postwar stucco bungalow out on the fringes of our little former lumber town.<br /><br />My grandmother believed in magic - White Magic. Back then she was like a Fairy Grandmother to me...<br /><br />One day during her visit, she took me by the hand and led me down to our unfinished basement. All she said to me was - ‘Today we’re going to visit the Zoo.’<br /><br />And she somehow, within my own private tiny toddler’s headspace, had suddenly transformed our little subterranean storage room - cluttered with bric-à-brac - by her very words, into a magical and mysterious Zoo.<br /><br />And just like the early and somewhat naïve Carlos Castaneda - self-hypnosis can work wonders - I SAW the wild animals in their exotic cages all around me!<br /><br />George MacDonald here conjures up a COMPLETELY FANCIFUL world for us too, in the bric-à-brac of an old, old house.<br /><br />Cause it’s a feeling that’s replete and satisfying in life to live close by a Deep, Bottomless Well of the Spirit in a palatial mansion on a princely property! <br /><br />So infers George MacDonald in Lilith.<br /><br />But his lucky hero inherits both...<br /><br />Well, in part only - for the mansion is a bit dark and decrepit. And it’s haunted. By unearthly beings.<br /><br />AND by a Secret World that dwells in a real spiritual opening. <br /><br />A secret world far more MYSTICAL - in the traditional sense - than Neil Gaiman’s! <br /><br />For MacDonald TRUSTS in Divine Providence.<br /><br />Just like I trusted my grandmother - and everyone else - when I was a kid. No wonder the Lord tells us to be like these little ones...<br /><br />It works wonders. It will take you back to that simple, first, miraculous and supernatural ORDINARINESS of MacDonald’s world.<br /><br />Now - follow MacDonald’s hero as he chases after his own familial White Rabbit - right Through the (Spiritual) Looking Glass, to a place of deep sleep and his soul’s ultimate replenishment. <br /><br />And we’ll ALL be in that place someday, with Faith.<br /><br />Such a world as this 19th century Scottish Man of the Cloth, the author, must have mentally envisioned, mayhaps, as he trudged the dreary miles to visit his snappish and dour parishioners on many a gloomy Highland day. <br /><br />Dreaming of the Rest that comes after a long life of labouring duty...<br /><br />And a Duty to which he, an eternal dreamer, was so temperamentally ill-suited!<br /><br />But his gloom is our gain.<br /><br />And THAT’s getting older for you - a time when shifting slivers of fitful dreams flit over our half-lit neutral autumnal mindscapes - like morning mist.<br /><br />But it gets even better as we age further - when we recover, as MacDonald does for us, our True Second Childhood - IF our dreams are born in a Milieu of Love.<br /><br />And we, the readers, find that WE are the ones who have been blessed in heaping, overflowing good measure, as we reap the reward he - along with our own daily acts of charity - have sowed for us.<br /><br />The reward of a bottomless reservoir of an endless wealth of imagination.<br /><br />And as it was for the young C.S. Lewis, discovering fantastical new worlds in MacDonald’s magical tales in his gloomy, grimy Irish preteen years, so it will be for us.<br /><br />Especially in the Great Beyond...<br /><br />So Five Gorgeous, Mystcal stars for this one!</span>
  <a data-text-id="16108780659342373929" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_5189722" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/5189722" />
    <a title="Mark Becher" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/314314-mark-becher"><img alt="Mark Becher" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/5189722?book_show_action=true">Aug 27, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mark Becher" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mark Becher" href="/user/show/314314-mark-becher">Mark Becher</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780802860613" href="/book/show/859561.Lilith">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer5189722" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11143985434020907982"> As my brother accurately described it, it starts out as a sort of Christian acid trip/Alice in Wonderland type experience. For the first half of the book you have almost no idea what is actually going on, but it's worth sticking it through because later it all falls into place. The story takes it's premise from an old Jewish myth about a companion named Lilith whom God gave to Adam before Eve. She was an angelic being, not human, and couldn't reconcile herself to the vocation of bearing Adam's </span>
  <span id="freeText11143985434020907982" style="display:none"> As my brother accurately described it, it starts out as a sort of Christian acid trip/Alice in Wonderland type experience. For the first half of the book you have almost no idea what is actually going on, but it's worth sticking it through because later it all falls into place. The story takes it's premise from an old Jewish myth about a companion named Lilith whom God gave to Adam before Eve. She was an angelic being, not human, and couldn't reconcile herself to the vocation of bearing Adam's children to populate the earth. They had a single child together before their relationship was abandoned and Lilith was replaced by a more fitting companion taken from Adam's side. Because of Eve's later fruitfulness Lilith bore an eternal hatred towards the human race. Most of the myth remains in the background however and the story centers on a man who stumbles through a mirror into an alternate realm where Adam, Eve, and Lilith continue to carry out their perpetual feud. The climax of the story involves Adam, the Father of mankind, and Mara, the Lady of Sorrows, leading Lilith grudgingly toward repentance. A parallel plot line involves the main character slowly learning how little it is he actually knows about what he has hitherto simply called "reality." Only after he willingly choosing to lie down in the House of the Dead is he able to rise to a fullness of life of which he has never before even dared to dream.<br /> C.S. Lewis has been quoted as saying that this book "baptized his imagination," and for good reason. The writing style leaves something to be desired (it is heavy and plodding in places), but the sheer myth of it is amazing. In my opinion, the entire story was worth the final few chapters which turn out to be a glimpse of what earthly life will be like after the resurrection of the dead. It is absolutely beautiful in its description. If you have yet to long for the world to come, this story may help take you a long way down that road. And, better yet, teach you to look for the glimpses of the heavenly kingdom which even now break through into the present world. Here is my favorite passage along those lines from Lilith:<br /><br />	<br />"Now and then, when I look round on my books, they seem to waver as if a wind rippled their solid mass, and another world were about to break through. Sometimes when I am abroad, a like thing takes place; the heavens and the earth, the trees and the grass appear for a moment to shake as if about to pass away; then, lo, they have settled again into the old familiar face! At times I seem to hear whisperings around me, as if some that loved me were talking of me; but when I would distinguish the words, they cease, and all is very still. I know not whether these things rise in my brain or enter it from without. I do not seek them, they come, and I let them go.<br /><br />"Strange dim memories, which will not abide identification, often, through the misty windows of the past, look out upon me in the broad daylight, but I never dream now. It may be, notwithstanding, that, when most awake, I am only dreaming the more! But when I wake at last into that life which, as a mother her child, carries this life in its bosom, I shall know that I wake, and shall doubt no more.<br /><br />"I wait; asleep or awake, I wait.<br /><br />"Our life is no dream, but it should and will perhaps become one."</span>
  <a data-text-id="11143985434020907982" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_691956" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/691956" />
    <a title="Danielle" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/48808-danielle"><img alt="Danielle" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1457753259p2/48808.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/691956?book_show_action=true">Apr 12, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Danielle" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Danielle" href="/user/show/48808-danielle">Danielle</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780802860613" href="/book/show/859561.Lilith">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Danielle by: </span>
      Tanya
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48808-danielle?shelf=a-lesson-in-sexism">a-lesson-in-sexism</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer691956" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14134799857961134706">I have an enormous respect for George MacDonald. His books such as <b>At the Back of the North Wind</b>, <b>The Princess and Curdie</b>, <b>The Princess and the Goblin</b>, <b>The Day Boy and the Night Girl</b> and even <b>Alec Forbes and His Friend Annie</b> were among my childhood favorites--they were magical and my first brushes with fantasy at 8-10 years old. He was an exceptionally gifted and inspired writer of the 1800's. I even respect his history as a clergyman who loved god but left off being a preacher because he believ</span>
  <span id="freeText14134799857961134706" style="display:none">I have an enormous respect for George MacDonald. His books such as <b>At the Back of the North Wind</b>, <b>The Princess and Curdie</b>, <b>The Princess and the Goblin</b>, <b>The Day Boy and the Night Girl</b> and even <b>Alec Forbes and His Friend Annie</b> were among my childhood favorites--they were magical and my first brushes with fantasy at 8-10 years old. He was an exceptionally gifted and inspired writer of the 1800's. I even respect his history as a clergyman who loved god but left off being a preacher because he believed, against the tenets of his times, that everyone was capable of redemption. Plus, he was Scottish. You know? :)<br /><br />But Lilith has been extremely difficult for me to get through. Lilith, the biblical Adam's 'other' wife, is basically damned and evil and vile because she's a <i>really bad mother.</i> (And I mean really bad, the characterization of which is misogynist in itself.) Almost worse, the main character's attraction to another <b>disturbingly child-like</b> girl is based solely on her having an intensely devoted mothering nature. This was "good". Anything else is "bad". <br /><br />Perhaps this is all because it's the first time I've attempted one of GM's books after leaving behind my childhood religion. Perhaps I'd find myself reacting the same way to any of my old favorites, at this point in my life. I find that despite my respect for him, I can't <b>not</b> mention the sexism inherent in it, even though he's a man of god from the 1800's--what else could you expect? I know he wasn't more misogynist than his contemporaries--and considering his many, many books from the feminine perspective, I suspect he was actually less so than most! But it's still worth mentioning to the modern reader that the preconceived notions of womanhood, especially <b>motherhood</b>, that this book is based on are absolutely revolting. <br /><br />It's pretty and romantic only if you are capable of completely divorcing the notion of womanhood and motherhood from any real, live woman. From humanity, from being capable of developing and living by our own moral compass, from the concept of self-determination. </span>
  <a data-text-id="14134799857961134706" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_347824778" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/347824778" />
    <a title="Dylan Jay Smith" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10200230-dylan-jay-smith"><img alt="Dylan Jay Smith" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1339615888p2/10200230.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/347824778?book_show_action=true">Jun 13, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dylan Jay Smith" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dylan Jay Smith" href="/user/show/10200230-dylan-jay-smith">Dylan Jay Smith</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer347824778" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer816042560485840249">This is by far one of the darkest books I've ever read. Coming from a Christian minister, I would expect the book to be a bit preachy. I found, however, that the story is way more of a dark fairy tale set in a somewhat biblical world, with faint biblical themes. It's hard, of course, not to be a bit biblical, considering some of the main characters are Adam, Eve, and Lilith (the first wife of Adam). MacDonald writes this story in a way that truly makes them characters in a book, rather than bibl</span>
  <span id="freeText816042560485840249" style="display:none">This is by far one of the darkest books I've ever read. Coming from a Christian minister, I would expect the book to be a bit preachy. I found, however, that the story is way more of a dark fairy tale set in a somewhat biblical world, with faint biblical themes. It's hard, of course, not to be a bit biblical, considering some of the main characters are Adam, Eve, and Lilith (the first wife of Adam). MacDonald writes this story in a way that truly makes them characters in a book, rather than bible superheroes here to promote proper Christian morals and whatnot. I'm an atheist, and never once felt uncomfortable, or as though I were being read a story from a minister.<br /><br />MacDonald uses amazing imagery when it comes to the world around Mr. Vane, the stories protagonist. At times things can be so beautiful, you wish you were there to see it, and then take a turn. There is a moment in the book where Mr. Vane is surrounded by flying, lit skulls, swirling around him in the black of night. MacDonald gives life to these skulls with an almost morbid reality. <br /><br />In short, this book is for everyone. If you have thought about giving it a go, don't let the religious undertones keep you away. It is a fantastic roller-coaster ride through what is living, what is good and evil, and what is hell. It will challenge even the most creative mind, and I dare you to read it and not think at least once, "Wow, I wish I had thought that up."</span>
  <a data-text-id="816042560485840249" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_156554519" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/156554519" />
    <a title="Karly Noelle White" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5059523-karly-noelle-white"><img alt="Karly Noelle White" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1531895779p2/5059523.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/156554519?book_show_action=true">Mar 25, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Karly Noelle White" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Karly Noelle White" href="/user/show/5059523-karly-noelle-white">Karly Noelle White</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5059523-karly-noelle-white?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer156554519" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16354924066865233185">George MacDonald is one of the most severely underrated authors of all time. A contemporary to Lewis Caroll and major influence on C.S. Lewis and J.R.R. Tolkien, this man’s imagination apparently knew no bounds and that is incredibly apparent in his masterpiece, Lilith. Narrated by a nameless everyman figure, it follows his adventures in a world he discovers after inheriting his father’s house and many unsettling circumstances there, leading him to a mirror which reveals another realm. Incredibl</span>
  <span id="freeText16354924066865233185" style="display:none">George MacDonald is one of the most severely underrated authors of all time. A contemporary to Lewis Caroll and major influence on C.S. Lewis and J.R.R. Tolkien, this man’s imagination apparently knew no bounds and that is incredibly apparent in his masterpiece, Lilith. Narrated by a nameless everyman figure, it follows his adventures in a world he discovers after inheriting his father’s house and many unsettling circumstances there, leading him to a mirror which reveals another realm. Incredibly complex, this story is really more like a series of events that the narrator witnesses in this realm, all leading down to a confrontation with Lilith, the legendary “first wife” of Adam in various cults of Jewish faith. Ultimately, it is a story of the nature of evil and the hope of redemption but it is packed with literary allusion and stunning imagery that the reader will not soon forget. Written with a deft wit and a finesse for the subtleties of human nature, MacDonald deserves to be in the ranks of he highest of fantasy and allegorical writers rather than collecting dust at the bottom of knowledge’s shelf. </span>
  <a data-text-id="16354924066865233185" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_382940806" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/382940806" />
    <a title="Christopher Bunn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4636657-christopher-bunn"><img alt="Christopher Bunn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1348432236p2/4636657.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/382940806?book_show_action=true">Aug 02, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Christopher Bunn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Christopher Bunn" href="/user/show/4636657-christopher-bunn">Christopher Bunn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer382940806" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16976136573236432442">I'm a fan of George MacDonald for his fantasies and for his children's books. His two older fantasies, Lilith and Phantases, are difficult to read and they're difficult to pigeon-hole. But why do we even want to pigeon-hole things in the first place? Oh, right. Marketing.<br/><br/>Anyway, like I said, Lilith is not the easiest book to read. Perhaps it's partially due to the era MacDonald was writing in, but he certainly isn't pandering to the lowest denominator here. The story is a haunting tale of a man </span>
  <span id="freeText16976136573236432442" style="display:none">I'm a fan of George MacDonald for his fantasies and for his children's books. His two older fantasies, Lilith and Phantases, are difficult to read and they're difficult to pigeon-hole. But why do we even want to pigeon-hole things in the first place? Oh, right. Marketing.<br /><br />Anyway, like I said, Lilith is not the easiest book to read. Perhaps it's partially due to the era MacDonald was writing in, but he certainly isn't pandering to the lowest denominator here. The story is a haunting tale of a man named Vane who travels to another reality where he learns about life and death and sin and redemption. That's the nutshell. The name Lilith, which refers to one of the characters in the story, is also the name given to Adam's first wife in traditional Jewish folklore (I think, if I have my facts straight).<br /><br />Part horror, part romance, part fantasy, part theological treatise, and part philosophical musing, Lilith has to be experienced for any true fantasy connoisseur. I won't guarantee that you'll like it, but I guarantee there are shining jewels in it that'll make you think or, at the least, make you uneasy.<br /><br />By the way, reading Lilith (and MacDonald's other fantasies) makes it fairly easy to see his influence on CS Lewis' fantasies. MacDonald is very much a thinker's fantasist. I'm combing through my memory files but I can't think of many who fall into that category (and they seem to become fewer and fewer, the closer the date approaches 2012). Voyage to Arcturus springs to mind, as well as Lewis's Space Trilogy, which I've always thought of as more fantasy than sci-fi, Chesterton's The Man Who Was Thursday, The Silmarillion. What others would qualify?<br /><br />Anyway, Lilith: difficult, maddening, puzzling, but definitely worth it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16976136573236432442" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_91960230" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/91960230" />
    <a title="Meg Powers" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2798717-meg-powers"><img alt="Meg Powers" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1506957766p2/2798717.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/91960230?book_show_action=true">Mar 01, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Meg Powers" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Meg Powers" href="/user/show/2798717-meg-powers">Meg Powers</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer91960230" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11789850738770076224">This was an interesting book to read after David Lindsay's <i>A Voyage to Arcturus</i>. Both deal with fantastic travel (<i>Lilith</i> with inter-dimensional travel, <i>Arcturus</i> with inter-planetary travel) as a means of religious and spiritual discovery. Both drag you on a harrowing journey, where many questions go unanswered. <i>Lilith</i>, however, is blatantly Christian. It is fun to read a fantasy novel that illustrates the milestones of Christianity, particularly Creation and the Resurrection, using quirky versio</span>
  <span id="freeText11789850738770076224" style="display:none">This was an interesting book to read after David Lindsay's <i>A Voyage to Arcturus</i>. Both deal with fantastic travel (<i>Lilith</i> with inter-dimensional travel, <i>Arcturus</i> with inter-planetary travel) as a means of religious and spiritual discovery. Both drag you on a harrowing journey, where many questions go unanswered. <i>Lilith</i>, however, is blatantly Christian. It is fun to read a fantasy novel that illustrates the milestones of Christianity, particularly Creation and the Resurrection, using quirky versions of Biblical characters. The Narnia series,which was hugely influenced by MacDonald, handles these ideas more elegantly through allegory and better writing, but <i>Lilith</i> is still an interesting read.<br /><br />The book is focused on death: living is life in death, every immoral action is a new death, death is actually life, etc. These philosophies are often delivered gravely by a talking raven, and in a confusing semantic manner similar to any other talking animal in a Lewis Carroll story. This gets a bit tedious and confusing,because MacDonald's writing juggles between clunky and to-the-point and lushly poetic. However, there were enough monsters,beautiful ladies,and mutilations to balance this out. <br /><br />The Narnia series has always been very important to me, so it was fun to read a book that so directly influenced C.S. Lewis. Hidden mundane objects in country houses used as portals to another world, speaking animals, fantastical Christian allegory-it's all in there. However, I don't think <i>Lilith</i> was intended as a children's novel, and it is interesting to see the contrast between these two books and and how each distilled their theological fantasies*. <i>Lilith</i> is mildly gothic, but certainly not as terrifying as Lewis's <i>The Last Battle</i>,which is downright apocalyptic.<br /><br />Personally, I imagined this book's world through a filter of cheesy BBC video quality, like the music video for the Cure's "Charlotte Sometimes" or an episode of Doctor Who. That's just me. I think it's because there is a lot of wandering through a British country house in the beginning.<br /><br />Anyway.<br /><br /><i>Lilith</i> is worth the read if you're a fan of C.S. Lewis and would like to see a direct influence. It's got some beautiful,solemn,creepy bits, and good descriptions of hideous beings. If you're remotely Catholic, it might freak you out a little. It triggered my ingrained Catholic terror of the afterlife. So I suppose this was a good choice for Lent. <br /><br /><br /><br /><br />*It's an experience similar to reading Lewis's Narnia series v.s. his Space Trilogy.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11789850738770076224" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1625293135" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1625293135" />
    <a title="Amanda G. Stevens" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9779665-amanda-g-stevens"><img alt="Amanda G. Stevens" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442981901p2/9779665.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1625293135?book_show_action=true">Apr 01, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Amanda G. Stevens" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Amanda G. Stevens" href="/user/show/9779665-amanda-g-stevens">Amanda G. Stevens</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780345017116" href="/book/show/1690826.Lilith">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1625293135" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5106948477844989783">What to say about this book? Well, it's the darker companion to <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/174948.Phantastes' title='Phantastes by George MacDonald' rel='nofollow noopener'>Phantastes</a>. It's an immersive fantasy dream-experience that transcends plot (though it has one). It's a Christian exhortation to the reader: die to self if you would live forever. It is by turns odd, humorous, witty, sweet, downright chilling, and glorious. It's often a blend of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/29797.The_Pilgrim_s_Progress' title='The Pilgrim's Progress by John Bunyan' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Pilgrim's Progress</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13023.Alice_in_Wonderland__Alice_s_Adventures_in_Wonderland___1_' title='Alice in Wonderland (Alice's Adventures in Wonderland, #1) by Lewis Carroll' rel='nofollow noopener'>Alice in Wonderland</a>, but I love it more than both those books put together. <br/><br/><i>Lilith</i> begins as a man called Vane steps through a mi</span>
  <span id="freeText5106948477844989783" style="display:none">What to say about this book? Well, it's the darker companion to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/174948.Phantastes" title="Phantastes by George MacDonald" rel="nofollow noopener">Phantastes</a>. It's an immersive fantasy dream-experience that transcends plot (though it has one). It's a Christian exhortation to the reader: die to self if you would live forever. It is by turns odd, humorous, witty, sweet, downright chilling, and glorious. It's often a blend of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/29797.The_Pilgrim_s_Progress" title="The Pilgrim's Progress by John Bunyan" rel="nofollow noopener">The Pilgrim's Progress</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13023.Alice_in_Wonderland__Alice_s_Adventures_in_Wonderland___1_" title="Alice in Wonderland (Alice's Adventures in Wonderland, #1) by Lewis Carroll" rel="nofollow noopener">Alice in Wonderland</a>, but I love it more than both those books put together. <br /><br /><i>Lilith</i> begins as a man called Vane steps through a mirror into a vividly detailed fantasy world. His guide is an old librarian who, in the alternate realm, appears as a raven and offers him both practical advice and spiritual challenges (and their arguments on metaphysics, not without wordplay, leave no doubt as to MacDonald's influence on <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/8164.Lewis_Carroll" title="Lewis Carroll" rel="nofollow noopener">Lewis Carroll</a>). Midway through the book, Vane's path crosses that of Lilith--yes, the same Lilith who, in Jewish mythology, was the rebellious first wife of Adam, replaced with Eve. <br /><br />As anyone who knows MacDonald will expect, the journeys of Vane and Lilith each illustrate the Christian's journey to redemption. He writes said journey with so many layers--of justice, mercy, sorrow, love for fellow man, willful sin vs. ignorant sin, mysteries vs. revelations of God. If all that sounds preachy, well, I never found the book to be so. I walked in the protagonist's footprints, saw the fantasy realm as he saw it, felt the pricking of his heart in my own. <br /><br />MacDonald wrote with a profound awareness of eternity I've never found in any other writer (except perhaps in the song lyrics of Rich Mullins). That bright and beautiful view is perhaps at its most resplendent in <i>Lilith</i>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5106948477844989783" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_111844238" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/111844238" />
    <a title="Veronica" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4014168-veronica"><img alt="Veronica" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1279245140p2/4014168.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/111844238?book_show_action=true">Jul 15, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Veronica" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Veronica" href="/user/show/4014168-veronica">Veronica</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer111844238" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12276048950360603701">'A long time we were together, I and the moon, walking side by side, she the dull shine, and I the live shadow.'<br/><br/><br/>I didn't like Lilith the first time I read it, despite being a big fan of MacDonald (and the people he influenced, like CS Lewis & Tolkien), but over the years as I have read it and read it again it has become one of my favorite books. Do not make the mistake of trying to understand each nuance- that would be like trying to understand all the symbolism of a Salvador Dali painting. Yes,</span>
  <span id="freeText12276048950360603701" style="display:none">'A long time we were together, I and the moon, walking side by side, she the dull shine, and I the live shadow.'<br /><br /><br />I didn't like Lilith the first time I read it, despite being a big fan of MacDonald (and the people he influenced, like CS Lewis &amp; Tolkien), but over the years as I have read it and read it again it has become one of my favorite books. Do not make the mistake of trying to understand each nuance- that would be like trying to understand all the symbolism of a Salvador Dali painting. Yes, the story is confusing, along the lines of Alice in Wonderland, and no, I honestly don't think MacDonald is a great writer. His power lies in his imagination and his ability to communicate realities about the human condition through these wild flights of fantasy. Lilith is ultimately a story about a terribly wicked woman and an ordinarily-selfish man who both find redemption through rest and sorrow, rather than their own strength. In between all this are desert monsters, skeleton dances, mirrors into other worlds, waking, sleeping, and a creepy librarian. Oh, not to mention MacDonald's weird poetry.<br /><br />Lilith and Phantastes are MacDonald's less-accessible books. If you haven't read MacDonald yet (shame on you) then you should start with The Princess and the Goblin or his fairy tales (Photogen and Nycteris is my favorite). Then move on to his masterpieces!</span>
  <a data-text-id="12276048950360603701" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer271232345" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1389118725706996954">This is one of these books that I -personally- would classify as solid 5/5.<br/>I only gave it a 4/5 for a single reason. MacDonald is not a typical writer. He was a priest, or, in the words of Wikipedia, a Christian minister.<br/>Why's that of importance? Because, the folk and the shepherds may use the same language, but not in the same way.<br/>The reader of this magnificent, allegoric, deeply religious fairytale will need to arm himself/herself with tons of good will to push onwards when the sentences becom</span>
  <span id="freeText1389118725706996954" style="display:none">This is one of these books that I -personally- would classify as solid 5/5.<br />I only gave it a 4/5 for a single reason. MacDonald is not a typical writer. He was a priest, or, in the words of Wikipedia, a Christian minister.<br />Why's that of importance? Because, the folk and the shepherds may use the same language, but not in the same way.<br />The reader of this magnificent, allegoric, deeply religious fairytale will need to arm himself/herself with tons of good will to push onwards when the sentences become tiresome and exhausting.<br />This is not something that the reader will realise halfway through the story. The very first paragraph reads: " I was impressed as if some ancient and altogether admirable and shining family had settled there in that part of the land called Concord, unknown to me,--to whom the sun was servant,-- who had not gone into society in the village,--who had not been called on". You get the point.<br />However, the story is impeccable. The characters are ever changing. The plot is masterfully weaved. This is indeed a fairytale of faith or a work of fiction, or fantasy, on religion. It is so much in fact, that not unless you take the time to read it, will you realise the depths and the widths to which this novel extends.<br />Even calling this a novel hurts its true nature.<br />Parallel words, creatures, embodiments of notions, respectable axioms, believed vices, valuable ethics... You need to savour this story to get to grips with what a truly holistic view of faith can produce.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1389118725706996954" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1021695641" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1920970025421432525">The second of MacDonald's adult fiction I've read. I come to them via C S Lewis's enthusiasm for his writings.<br/><br/>It's been said of Lewis, as writer, teacher & a conversationalist, that his own love & enthusiasm for certain books & authors could be infectious & send readers & listeners away eager to read works which then proved to be disappointing to them, wondering what he saw in them.<br/><br/>This is partly true of my response to "Lilith". There are many weaknesses in the plot & style of this book that are</span>
  <span id="freeText1920970025421432525" style="display:none">The second of MacDonald's adult fiction I've read. I come to them via C S Lewis's enthusiasm for his writings.<br /><br />It's been said of Lewis, as writer, teacher &amp; a conversationalist, that his own love &amp; enthusiasm for certain books &amp; authors could be infectious &amp; send readers &amp; listeners away eager to read works which then proved to be disappointing to them, wondering what he saw in them.<br /><br />This is partly true of my response to "Lilith". There are many weaknesses in the plot &amp; style of this book that are very trying. Three will do as examples.<br /><br />1) His treatment of innocence &amp; goodness as characterized in the Little Ones. He shows all worst elements of the Victorian period in this area. The tweeness of his presentation these characters, the baby-talk &amp; behaviours are equal to the most sick-making of Dickens' heroines e.g. Dora Copperfield.<br /><br />2) Some terribly heavy-handed clumsy use of symbols to represent ideas made reading this like eating very doughy bread.<br /><br />3) The excursions into the language of the Authorized version in conversations between Vane &amp; Adam, Eve etc. just did not work.<br /><br />However, despite these &amp; many other short-comings there were also some wonderful aspects to his story, some really inventive fantasy &amp; many thoughts well worth underling &amp; remembering. </span>
  <a data-text-id="1920970025421432525" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer13289599018087328093">It's hard to weird me out, and being so bizarro as to have exceeded my comprehension level is a rare feat. If I could finish this it would probably be a four star. Starts out lovely but becomes disconnected and hard to follow. If you liked, "Dream Quest of Unknown Kadath," you know the sort of dedication it takes to finish this. A good writer entertaining his edgiest explorations. I might revisit this, but my to read pile has too many abandoned books at the moment. DNF.<br/> </span>

          </span>

<div id="review_697099068" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/697099068" />
    <a title="M." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9075047-m"><img alt="M." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442965983p2/9075047.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/697099068?book_show_action=true">Aug 17, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="M." class="user" itemprop="url" name="M." href="/user/show/9075047-m">M.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer697099068" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3042969610811571750">George MacDonald stands apart singularly in my reading experiences. C.S. Lewis said that every page he wrote plagiarized MacDonald's ideas, and also admitted that MacDonald was not a great writer in the quality of his prose. I agree fully on the later and see his point with the former. <br/><br/>In his favor, MacDonald's adult fantasy work is great reading because he has the most distinctly confident and original ideas for myths that I have ever read. His fantastic worlds are wholly original and inspired.</span>
  <span id="freeText3042969610811571750" style="display:none">George MacDonald stands apart singularly in my reading experiences. C.S. Lewis said that every page he wrote plagiarized MacDonald's ideas, and also admitted that MacDonald was not a great writer in the quality of his prose. I agree fully on the later and see his point with the former. <br /><br />In his favor, MacDonald's adult fantasy work is great reading because he has the most distinctly confident and original ideas for myths that I have ever read. His fantastic worlds are wholly original and inspired. His spiritual and philosophical components require deep mediation and are generally inspiring and quotable. <br /><br />On the other hand, both due to the period of the writing and to his own verbose and exhausting communication, passages can drone and quickly lose the modern reader's interest.<br /><br />Overall, Lilith is well worth a read. It deals with alternate planes, world mythologies and religion, and fleshes out the concepts of good, evil, forgiveness and restoration in a way that contains real elements of horror and exquisite visions of recreation. It takes some work, but I believe I shall mull upon it for many years.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3042969610811571750" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer66756844" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer5370009360559951337">This is perhaps my favorite book of all time. It does not get enough good press. It is definitely MacDonald's magnum opus. I would recommend it to all fantasy-lovers and readers just looking for something refreshingly different. Like most of George MacDonald's work, Lilith does have strong religious undertones, but they are presented in a unique way that I don't believe will offend or even distract non-religious readers. The religious content is comparable to that in the works of CS Lewis. I pro</span>
  <span id="freeText5370009360559951337" style="display:none">This is perhaps my favorite book of all time. It does not get enough good press. It is definitely MacDonald's magnum opus. I would recommend it to all fantasy-lovers and readers just looking for something refreshingly different. Like most of George MacDonald's work, Lilith does have strong religious undertones, but they are presented in a unique way that I don't believe will offend or even distract non-religious readers. The religious content is comparable to that in the works of CS Lewis. I promise this book will be unlike anything you've ever read before or will ever read again. I would highly recommend all of MacDonald's fantasy works, but Lilith is definitely my favorite. I would welcome discussion on this book with anyone who has read it!</span>

<div id="review_428650377" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/428650377" />
    <a title="Matthew Hodge" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13404196-matthew-hodge"><img alt="Matthew Hodge" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601552854p2/13404196.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/428650377?book_show_action=true">Oct 06, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matthew Hodge" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matthew Hodge" href="/user/show/13404196-matthew-hodge">Matthew Hodge</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13404196-matthew-hodge?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13404196-matthew-hodge?shelf=hard-copy">hard-copy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13404196-matthew-hodge?shelf=christian">christian</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer428650377" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10532691231916181221">I'm almost at a loss how to review this one. The thing I was really glad about is that there was an introduction in the one that I had by C.S. Lewis, which put me in the right headspace to read this book. Lewis explained that MacDonald was not necessarily a great *writer*. What he was, though, was an exceptional myth-maker. In the same way, that we don't necessarily think of a particular writer when we think of Greek mythology, rather we think of the stories themselves - in the same way, MacDona</span>
  <span id="freeText10532691231916181221" style="display:none">I'm almost at a loss how to review this one. The thing I was really glad about is that there was an introduction in the one that I had by C.S. Lewis, which put me in the right headspace to read this book. Lewis explained that MacDonald was not necessarily a great *writer*. What he was, though, was an exceptional myth-maker. In the same way, that we don't necessarily think of a particular writer when we think of Greek mythology, rather we think of the stories themselves - in the same way, MacDonald's stories are not great because of the writing itself, but the power of the concept that he is trying to get across.<br /><br />Now that I've read the entire book, I see what Lewis is trying to get at. And it is this willingness to put up with a rambling story that is, ultimately, quite allegorical, that might make the difference between whether you love it or hate it.<br /><br />The story, such as it is, tells of Mr Vane, a young man who inherits a rambling country house with a large book room and discovers a way into another world. The first half of the book, in which he explores the world, I found surreal and disorienting. I had no idea what the symbolism meant, or where the story was going. I just had to trust that it would all work out.<br /><br />I'm glad that I did, because when I got to the second half, the threads started to come together. In the end, it's a very metaphysical story about good and evil, and especially the concept of dying in order to truly live. Belief in a life after this one is a fairly standard part of Christian belief, but how often do we sit around thinking about it?<br /><br />Meanwhile, MacDonald has meditated on it, and in his own poetic fantastical way, paints a picture of life after death in a way which is neither angels with syrup, or just a rough concept. Those of you who have read C.S. Lewis' "The Last Battle" will recognise some of the ideas that have been borrowed.<br /><br />In the end, despite the difficulty of the read and its shortcomings as a fantasy novel (which is what it most resembles), I think it succeeds in leaving you with a glimpse of something beyond this world, which is its most appealing aspect.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10532691231916181221" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review428650377">

<div id="review_100171066" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/100171066" />
    <a title="Jeslyn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2566227-jeslyn"><img alt="Jeslyn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1248981341p2/2566227.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/100171066?book_show_action=true">Apr 26, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeslyn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeslyn" href="/user/show/2566227-jeslyn">Jeslyn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781603862059" href="/book/show/7186698-lilith">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer100171066" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10466558141433864831">This is the third novel I've read by George MacDonald, the first being The Princess and The Goblin, sort of a young-adult novel, which was wonderfully written. I then started searching out other titles and now have a little collection. <br/><br/>Frankly, I was a bit worried in the beginning - it started very reminiscent of Phantastes, and was loaded with exclamation points, which seemed odd...but don't be fooled: the story picks up in a hurry, and is an excellent read. MacDonald's imaginings of the world </span>
  <span id="freeText10466558141433864831" style="display:none">This is the third novel I've read by George MacDonald, the first being The Princess and The Goblin, sort of a young-adult novel, which was wonderfully written. I then started searching out other titles and now have a little collection. <br /><br />Frankly, I was a bit worried in the beginning - it started very reminiscent of Phantastes, and was loaded with exclamation points, which seemed odd...but don't be fooled: the story picks up in a hurry, and is an excellent read. MacDonald's imaginings of the world after death, fulfillment of biblical promises, and descriptions of our temporal understandings and behavior make this a story of great depth and scope - every once in a while I'm struck by what an enormous tale an author has crafted, not even in the number of pages, but the scope of vision that they have: did they come up with the "general idea" first and, if so, how much of the tale was the "general idea"; or did they take a small thought and begin to weave other things into it as they went along? At any rate, some authors amaze me with their vision, and MacDonald is one of them. It is a page turner, without question, but a story that had me pondering throughout, and finishing with joy that I had read it. <br /><br />Both C. S. Lewis and J. R. R. Tolkien credit George MacDonald as their inspiration to become authors, and it is easy to see that in his writings. <br /><br />Favorite quote from the book: "That which is within a man, not that which lies beyond his vision, is the main factor in what is about to befall him: the operation upon him is the event."<br /><br />In spite of the exuberant use of exclamation points at the outset, it still ranks 5 stars to me.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10466558141433864831" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_602882673" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/602882673" />
    <a title="John" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2095414-john"><img alt="John" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1317221772p2/2095414.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/602882673?book_show_action=true">Apr 29, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="John" class="user" itemprop="url" name="John" href="/user/show/2095414-john">John</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780802860613" href="/book/show/859561.Lilith">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2095414-john?shelf=2013">2013</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer602882673" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14289007259744753098">MacDonald was a big influence on C.S. Lewis, as many know. Lewis acknowledges this in the preface to this edition. MacDonald was a universalist, which is quite apparent in reading Lilith. The entire premise of the book requires one to enter a world where universalism is presupposed. Universalism, at least the MacDonald version of it, believes that all people will eventually bow the knee to Christ in faith, repent, believe, and enter into the New Heavens and the New Earth. MacDonald even argues t</span>
  <span id="freeText14289007259744753098" style="display:none">MacDonald was a big influence on C.S. Lewis, as many know. Lewis acknowledges this in the preface to this edition. MacDonald was a universalist, which is quite apparent in reading Lilith. The entire premise of the book requires one to enter a world where universalism is presupposed. Universalism, at least the MacDonald version of it, believes that all people will eventually bow the knee to Christ in faith, repent, believe, and enter into the New Heavens and the New Earth. MacDonald even argues that Satan himself will be redeemed. MacDonald argues that it isn't good enough for evil to be separated from good--in Heaven and Hell, but evil must be changed to good for good to triumph. This is philosophical more than theological.<br /><br />In any case, the book itself is a highly imaginative, and largely cryptic work. The first half of the book is difficult, slow, mystical, and almost non-sensical. You've got to stick with it to the end to start to understand what has happened in the first half. It took discipline for me to stick through it, and I can understand why many wouldn't do so.<br /><br />The end is fascinating and very interesting. When it is made clear who the main characters really are it gets good. But frankly, I don't think it really redeems the first half of the book. C.S. Lewis fans will likely want to read this to understand MacDonald's influence on Lewis, but I can't recommend it to others.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14289007259744753098" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/42322623?book_show_action=true">Jan 08, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kj" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kj" href="/user/show/298072-kj">Kj</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780802860613" href="/book/show/859561.Lilith">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/298072-kj?shelf=own-it">own-it</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/298072-kj?shelf=seattle-school-of-theology">seattle-school-of-theology</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/298072-kj?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer42322623" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10625838477285603893">Incandescent and blazingly truthful, George MacDonald's 1895 fantasy on Life after life, is not just a book, its a portal. To what end that portal leads, I will leave the reader to discover for themself. As the author C.S. Lewis credits as having "baptized his imagination," MacDonald's influence on the creation of Narnia is not difficult to locate in "Lillith". Likewise, I can only assume that J.M. Barrie, a fellow Scotsman, was influenced by MacDonald's writing as he formed his Never-land chara</span>
  <span id="freeText10625838477285603893" style="display:none">Incandescent and blazingly truthful, George MacDonald's 1895 fantasy on Life after life, is not just a book, its a portal. To what end that portal leads, I will leave the reader to discover for themself. As the author C.S. Lewis credits as having "baptized his imagination," MacDonald's influence on the creation of Narnia is not difficult to locate in "Lillith". Likewise, I can only assume that J.M. Barrie, a fellow Scotsman, was influenced by MacDonald's writing as he formed his Never-land characters. Lona and the Little Ones are (possibly literally) the eschatological realization of Wendy and the Lost Boys. <br /><br />Yet for all the evocation of environments and devices similar to those found in Alice and Wonderland and the Chronicles of Narnia, this book is not for children. It may be for the childlike, but essentially, it's an undaunted expedition into and out of the depths of cowardice and narcissism. In other words, this should be required reading in every seminary program. (Lucky for me, my seminary did assign it. Thanks Chelle)<br /><br />"Lillith" takes some work to get through, but the language and imagery will sate a thirst you did not yet know you had. <br /><br />If you thought the Wardrobe opened up a crazy world, try Mr. Vane's bookshelf...<br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10625838477285603893" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1512824294" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1512824294" />
    <a title="David " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/502680-david"><img alt="David " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1206799125p2/502680.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1512824294?book_show_action=true">Jan 16, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David " class="user" itemprop="url" name="David " href="/user/show/502680-david">David </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/502680-david?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/502680-david?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1512824294" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7857525602130044217">George Macdonald certainly had an imagination. This book takes the reader on a journey, alongside Mr. Vane, similar in some ways to other journey stories from MacDonald's own Phantastes to Pilgrims Progress or The Hobbit. Aren't all these stories similar - leaving home, entering the unknown, learning about yourself and the world, finding redemption. <br/><br/>I find MacDonald in this book, like Phantastes, somewhat difficult to read. Perhaps it is that the style is so different from later fantasy a la Tol</span>
  <span id="freeText7857525602130044217" style="display:none">George Macdonald certainly had an imagination. This book takes the reader on a journey, alongside Mr. Vane, similar in some ways to other journey stories from MacDonald's own Phantastes to Pilgrims Progress or The Hobbit. Aren't all these stories similar - leaving home, entering the unknown, learning about yourself and the world, finding redemption. <br /><br />I find MacDonald in this book, like Phantastes, somewhat difficult to read. Perhaps it is that the style is so different from later fantasy a la Tolkien and Lewis. Or it is that the journey is not simply through one geographical land that is somewhat defined (like Middle Earth) but is through different dimensions in an undefined landscape. In other words, much more mythological.<br /><br />Most intriguing about this book is that it is the story of redemption of Lillith, Adam's first wife whom he rejected because she was evil. I could not help but think, as Lillith found redemption, that this made sense in MacDonald's hopeful worldview. After all, he was a universalist who believed all people would be redeemed. Interesting he chose Lillith and not some other figure to illustrate this. Overall, an interesting and thought provoking story.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7857525602130044217" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1603826804?book_show_action=true">Mar 16, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Hope" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Hope" href="/user/show/13246091-hope">Hope</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780802860613" href="/book/show/859561.Lilith">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13246091-hope?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13246091-hope?shelf=christianity">christianity</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1603826804" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13082173511111056537">Not since studying Flannery O'Connor have I read anything so bewildering and mesmerizing at the same time. But with O'Connor I had a college professor explaining some of the most confusing images.<br/><br/>George MacDonald does a masterful job of weaving important themes into this fantasy novel: life, death, sin, redemption, dreams and reality, but I was often confused with the symbolism. What did the white leopard represent? the spotted leopard? the white princess? the shadow? the children? Occasionally </span>
  <span id="freeText13082173511111056537" style="display:none">Not since studying Flannery O'Connor have I read anything so bewildering and mesmerizing at the same time. But with O'Connor I had a college professor explaining some of the most confusing images.<br /><br />George MacDonald does a masterful job of weaving important themes into this fantasy novel: life, death, sin, redemption, dreams and reality, but I was often confused with the symbolism. What did the white leopard represent? the spotted leopard? the white princess? the shadow? the children? Occasionally I got glimpses of hidden meanings.<br /><br />Frankly, I would not have persevered with the book if it has not been for its famous connection to C.S. Lewis who was influenced by it. I was comforted to read a letter from Lewis to his friend Arthur Greeves, saying that he only began to understand the layers of meaning in Lilith when he read it a second time. </span>
  <a data-text-id="13082173511111056537" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/10838830?book_show_action=true">Dec 21, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Amanda" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Amanda" href="/user/show/706272-amanda">Amanda</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer10838830" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13099830056250435019">Much, much preachier and more metaphysical than Phantastes, this MacDonald fantasy appealed less to me but still kept me reading so that I finished all 250 pages in one sitting. In this book, MacDonald's love affair with death gets even heavier and creepier. He also seems to be making a commentary on the danger of the "New Woman," who willfully abandoned the role of "Angel in the House" at the turn of the century. The moments wherein he becomes wrapped up in the world-building aspect of the fant</span>
  <span id="freeText13099830056250435019" style="display:none">Much, much preachier and more metaphysical than Phantastes, this MacDonald fantasy appealed less to me but still kept me reading so that I finished all 250 pages in one sitting. In this book, MacDonald's love affair with death gets even heavier and creepier. He also seems to be making a commentary on the danger of the "New Woman," who willfully abandoned the role of "Angel in the House" at the turn of the century. The moments wherein he becomes wrapped up in the world-building aspect of the fantasy and stops proselytizing are by far the most enjoyable parts of the book for me.</span>
